Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/asset/images/medium/am3c19126_0008.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/asset/images/large/am3c19126_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c19126&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">The ether-based electrolytes show excellent performance on anodes in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), but they still show poor compatibility with the cathodes. Here, ether electrolytes with NaBF<sub>4</sub> as the main salt or additive were applied in NFM//HC full cells and showed enhanced performance than the electrolyte with NaPF<sub>6</sub>. Then, BF<sub>4</sub><sup>–</sup> was found to have a stronger interaction with Na<sup>+</sup>, which could reduce the solvation of Na<sup>+</sup> with the solvent, thus inducing the formation of the cathode electrolyte interface (CEI) and solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layers rich in inorganic species. Moreover, the morphology, structure, composition, and solubility of CEI and SEI were explored, concluding that NaBF<sub>4</sub> could induce more stable CEI and SEI layers rich in B-containing species and inorganics. This work proposes using NaBF<sub>4</sub> as the main salt or additive to improve the performance of ether electrolytes in NFM//HC full cells, which provides a strategy to improve the compatibility of ether-based electrolytes and cathodes.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D11585%26pageCount%3D10%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DXiaoli%2BYi%252C%2BXinhai%2BLi%252C%2BJing%2BZhong%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D16%26issueNum%3D9%26contentID%3Dacsami.3c19126%26title%3DBF4%25E2%2580%2593%2BTailoring%2BSolvation%2BChemistry%2Bof%2BEther-Based%2BElectrolytes%2Bto%2BConstruct%2BStable%2BElectrode%252FElectrolyte%2BInterfaces%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BFull%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D10%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D11594%26publicationDate%3DMarch%2B2024" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2024 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291952&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrochemical cells" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrochemical%20cells" class="article__tags__link">Electrochemical cells</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292120&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrolytes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrolytes" class="article__tags__link">Electrolytes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292200&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Layers" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Layers" class="article__tags__link">Layers</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292274&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Surface chemistry" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Surface%20chemistry" class="article__tags__link">Surface chemistry</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Ether+Electrolytes&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_ether%20electrolytes">ether electrolytes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Nabf4&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_NaBF4">NaBF<sub>4</sub></a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Napf6&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_NaPF6">NaPF<sub>6</sub></a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Electrolyte+Additive&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_electrolyte%20additive">electrolyte additive</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted much attention in recent years due to the abundance and wide distribution of Na reserves. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2" aria-label="reference 1 and 2">(1,2)</a> The advantages of low cost make the application of SIBs in the field of large-scale energy storage systems and low-speed electric vehicles more promising. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref3" aria-label="reference 3">(3)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The cathodes, anodes, and electrolytes are the three most important components of SIBs, and the stability of the interface between the electrode and electrolyte directly determines the energy density, stability, and safety of batteries. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4 ref5 ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5 ref6" aria-label="reference 4 to 6">(4−6)</a> However, most researches were focused on the materials chemistry, and the researches about electrolytes are not so eye-catching. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7" aria-label="reference 7">(7)</a> It is noteworthy that the stability of the electrode/electrolyte interface is closely related to the electrolyte composition and solvation structures. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4 ref5 ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5 ref8" aria-label="reference 4, 5 and 8">(4,5,8)</a> Therefore, understanding and regulating the solvation structures of the electrolytes are important ways to construct stable electrode/electrolyte interfaces.</div><div class="NLM_p">The commonly used nonaqueous organic electrolytes mainly include ethers, carbonates, carboxylic esters, and so forth. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> In recent years, many studies have found that ether-based electrolytes can form more stable solid electrolyte interface (SEI) films and show better cycle and rate capability than ester-based electrolytes when applied to several anode materials such as hard carbon, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10 ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 10 and 11">(10,11)</a> graphite, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12 ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 12 and 13">(12,13)</a> and alloying anodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> However, ether-based electrolytes are rarely used in cathode materials and the corresponding full cells. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> This is mainly because the ether-based electrolytes exhibit poorer compatibility with cathodes than the carbonate-based electrolyte. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref16" aria-label="reference 9 and 16">(9,16)</a> Therefore, improving the compatibility between ether-based electrolytes and positive electrodes can promote the application of ether-based electrolytes in the field of sodium-ion batteries. Tetraglyme has been applied in sodium-ion full batteries in some peer-reviewed papers because of its better oxidation stability among ether solvents; however, the electrochemical performance is still not so satisfactory. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15 ref17 ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref17 ref18" aria-label="reference 15, 17 and 18">(15,17,18)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">NaPF<sub>6</sub> has become one of the most widely used sodium salts in electrolytes because of its ability to produce more conductive electrolytes and smaller interface impedance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> However, NaPF<sub>6</sub> suffers from the safety issue caused by hydrolysis and pyrolysis. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> In addition, some studies have found that the performance of full cells with high-potential cathodes is unsatisfactory when NaPF<sub>6</sub> is applied with ether electrolytes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> Hu et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> found that the anode-free Na battery with a layered oxide-based Na[Cu<sub>1/9</sub>Ni<sub>2/9</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> cathode displayed low cycling efficiencies and continuous depletion of active Na content when 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in diglyme electrolyte was used. Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub>//HC full cell with 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in diglyme electrolyte was also explored by Johansson et al. and showed rapid capacity fading, which is attributed to unstable SEI layers. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> In our previous work, we applied 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> tetraglyme in various battery systems with different operation ranges. It is worth mentioning that the deterioration of ether electrolytes closely related to the “redox shuttle” between cathodes and low-potential anodes was found for the first time. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> Here, the “redox shuttle” effect is that some soluble intermediate products from low-potential anodes (&lt;0.57 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) would shuttle to the cathodes (&gt;3.45 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) and be oxidized. Therefore, constructing stable cathode electrolyte interface (CEI) films on cathodes and SEI layers on anodes is the key to promoting the application of ether-based electrolytes in full cells with high-potential cathodes.</div><div class="NLM_p">NaBF<sub>4</sub> is another commonly used sodium salt, which is also widely used in the fields of chemistry, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24 ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 24 and 25">(24,25)</a> biotechnology, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> pharmaceutical industry, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> and so forth. Compared with NaPF<sub>6</sub>, NaBF<sub>4</sub> has a lower cost and shows outstanding performance when applied to ether-based electrolytes. Palani Balaya et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> reported that 1 M NaBF<sub>4</sub> in tetraglyme is thermally stable and could induce a stable SEI layer when applied to the Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> material. Zi-Feng Ma et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> found that a nonflammable electrolyte with NaBF<sub>4</sub> as the sodium salt could significantly stabilize the cyclability of NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub>//HC full cells. It has also been reported that 1 M NaBF<sub>4</sub> in diglyme could realize the stable cycling of Na metal anode. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> Thus, it is very meaningful to explore the effect of NaBF<sub>4</sub> on the inhibition of the “redox shuttle” effect. In addition, due to the low solubility and poor Na<sup>+</sup> conductivity of NaBF<sub>4</sub> in electrolytes, if a small amount of NaBF<sub>4</sub> can significantly improve the interfacial stability, then this strategy can achieve a universal application. Therefore, it is also necessary to explore the effect of NaBF<sub>4</sub> as an ancillary salt on the interfacial stability and inhibition of “redox shuttle.”</div><div class="NLM_p last">In this work, the ether electrolytes with NaPF<sub>6</sub> (PG) and NaBF<sub>4</sub> (BG) as salts were applied on NFM//HC full cells, and it was found that BG shows more excellent performance. To further explore the effect of NaBF<sub>4</sub> as an ancillary salt on the interface stability, we designed an electrolyte with 0.1 M NaBF<sub>4</sub> and 0.9 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> (PB91) and also obtained excellent performance as BG. In addition, a serious self-discharge capacity loss of PG could be inhibited by BG and PB91. Through studying the effect of NaBF<sub>4</sub> on the morphology, structure, composition, and solubility of CEI layers on NFM cathodes and SEI layers on HC anodes, it was found that the excellent performance of BG and PB91 could be attributed to the more stable CEI and SEI layers rich in B-containing species and inorganics. The stronger interaction between BF<sub>4</sub><sup>–</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> was unraveled by <sup>23</sup>Na NMR and desolvation energy calculation, which could reduce the solvation of Na<sup>+</sup> with the solvent, thus inducing the formation of CEI and SEI layers rich in inorganic species.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Chemicals and Materials</h3><div class="NLM_p last">NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NFM) was purchased from Guangdong Greenway Technology Co., Ltd. Hard carbon (HC) material was purchased from KAIJIN New Energy Co., Ltd., China. Sodium hexafluorophosphate (NaPF<sub>6</sub>) was purchased from Do-fluoride Chemicals Co., Ltd., China; sodium tetrafluoroborate (NaBF<sub>4</sub>) was purchased from Shanghai Titan Scientific Co., Ltd. (Adamas-beta); and tetraethylene glycol dimethyl ether (G4) was purchased from Duoduo Chemical Technology Co., Ltd., China.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Electrode and Electrolyte Preparation</h3><div class="NLM_p">To prepare NFM electrodes, NFM material, carbon black (super P), and polyvinylidene difluoride (PVDF) at a weight ratio of 95:3.5:1.5 were mixed with an appropriate amount of <i>N</i>-methyl pyrrolidone (NMP) to form a homogeneous slurry. HC electrodes were prepared by mixing HC material, Super P, sodium carboxymethyl cellulose (CMC), and styrene–butadiene rubber (SBR) at a weight ratio of 95:1:1.5:2.5; then, an appropriate amount of H<sub>2</sub>O was added and stirred to obtain a homogeneous slurry. Then, the slurry was cast onto an Al foil and dried at 90 °C in a vacuum oven for 12 h, and the material was cut into disks with diameters of 12 mm.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The electrolytes were prepared by dissolving 1 M (mol L<sup>–1</sup>) NaPF<sub>6</sub>/NaBF<sub>4</sub> or 0.9 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> + 0.1 M NaBF<sub>4</sub> into G4 to obtain 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in G4 electrolyte (PG), 1 M NaBF<sub>4</sub> in G4 electrolyte (BG), and 0.9 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> + 0.1 M NaBF<sub>4</sub> in G4 electrolyte (PB91). All electrolytes were prepared in a glovebox, with the moisture and oxygen contents below 0.1 ppm. Coin cells (CR2032) were assembled in a glovebox, and GF/A glass filters impregnated with 80 μL of electrolyte were applied as separators.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Electrode and Electrolyte Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The surface morphology and element content determination of NFM and HC electrodes were explored by scanning electron microscopy (SEM, EOL, JSM-5612LV) and energy-dispersive spectroscopy technique (EDS). The content of traditional metal element was determined by an inductively coupled plasma atomic emission spectrometer (ICP, PerkinElmer, Avio 500). The elemental composition of CEI and SEI layers with Al Kα radiation (<i>hv</i> = 1486.6 eV) was investigated by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) (Thermo Fisher, ESCALAB 250Xi). A differential electrochemical mass spectrometer (DEMS, HPR-40, Hiden) with high-purity He (99.999%) as a carrier gas at a flow rate of 0.75 mL min<sup>–1</sup> was used to explore the gas evolution of NFM//HC full cells. All electrodes obtained from cycled cells were washed with the DME solvent before the characterization of SEM and XPS. A liquid nuclear magnetic resonance instrument (NMR) (Bruker AVANCE NEO 400M) was used to obtain the <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectrum. The solubility of CEI and SEI layers was calculated by the capacity loss of Al//Na and Cu//Na cells with different pause times. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> The ionic conductivity was tested by a conductivity meter (METTLER S230) at 25 °C.</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7">2.4.  Electrochemical Measurement</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Galvanostatic charge/discharge tests were conducted by a Neware battery test system. The rate of 1 C corresponds to 120 mA g<sup>–1</sup> for NFM electrodes. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) was performed by an electrochemical workstation (Admiral, 2245w). Chronoamperometry tests were conducted after the NFM//HC full cells were charged to 4.0 V at 0.2 C, and then the batteries were held at 4.0 V for 5 h. The electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements were performed in a frequency range from 500 kHz to 10 mHz.</div></div><div id="sec2_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8">2.5.  Calculation Methods</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The molecular dynamics simulations were conducted in the Forcite module by Materials Studio of Accelrys Inc., and all the dynamics simulations used the COMPASS II force field. The PG electrolyte model box contains 50 NaPF<sub>6</sub> and 227 G4, the BG model contains 50 NaBF<sub>4</sub> and 227 G4, and the PB91 electrolyte model contains 45 NaPF<sub>6</sub>, 5 NaBF<sub>4</sub>, and 227 G4. First, all electrolyte systems were initially equilibrated at 600–300 K in the NVT ensemble for 10 cycles to obtain the optimized structure with the lowest energy. Then, the electrolytes were equilibrated in the isothermal–isobaric ensemble (constant NPT) using the Berendsen barostat to maintain a pressure of 0.0001 GPa for 300 ps. The products were obtained by the canonical ensemble (NVT) running 500 ps at 298 K. Finally, dynamic simulation of the optimized model was conducted to obtain the radial distribution function (<i>g</i>(<i>r</i>)) and coordination number distribution (<i>n</i>(<i>r</i>)). Density functional theory (DFT) calculation was applied to calculate the solvation free energies.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">To explore the performance of ether electrolytes with NaPF<sub>6</sub> and NaBF<sub>4</sub> when applied in high-potential cathodes, NFM cathode with a high operation potential (4.2 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) was selected. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a, the NFM//Na cell with the NaPF<sub>6</sub> G4-based electrolyte (PG) exhibits poor cycling performance and low Coulombic efficiencies. After 100 cycles at 0.2 C, the specific capacity of PG decreases from 149.7 to 58.9 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, and the average Coulombic efficiency is as low as 96.15%. From our previous study, the low-efficiency phenomenon of PG was attributed to the “redox shuttle” effect of ether electrolytes between cathodes and low-potential anodes, which would lead to the rapid deterioration of batteries. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> However, when NaPF<sub>6</sub> is replaced by NaBF<sub>4</sub> (BG), the cycling performance and efficiencies were promoted obviously, and the capacity could remain 83.0 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 100 cycles. Interestingly, when a small amount of NaBF<sub>4</sub> (0.1 M) was mixed with NaPF<sub>6</sub> (0.9 M) in G4 to obtain PB91, the cycling performance and efficiencies could be improved significantly as BG, which implies that a small amount of NaBF<sub>4</sub> could induce more stable SEI layers. The cycling performance at 1 C also shows the same result (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/suppl_file/am3c19126_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>). The first charge and discharge profiles of NFM//Na cells with three electrolytes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b) display that PG shows an obvious side reaction platform corresponding to the shuttle effect at ∼3.5 V, which leads to a low initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 71.2%. However, BG and PB91 do not show the side reaction platform; thus, the ICEs are improved remarkably to 83.9% and 87.0%, respectively. This result further indicates that NaBF<sub>4</sub> could suppress the side reaction of PG. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) tests were also conducted to explore the difference of PG, BG, and PB91 electrolytes (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/suppl_file/am3c19126_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). The initial CV curves of NFM//Na further manifest that BG and PB91 could inhibit the side reactions of ether electrolytes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c). Due to the structural instability of NFM materials, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> materials with a far more stable structure than NFM were also applied to assess the effect of PG and PB91 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/suppl_file/am3c19126_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>). The Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>//Na cell with PG electrolytes fails at 319 cycles, and the battery shows low cycling efficiency, which is also attributed to the ‘redox shuttle’ effect. However, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>//Na in PB91 could stably run 1700 cycles with high efficiencies and maintain a capacity retention rate as high as 84.52%. These results further indicate that NaBF<sub>4</sub> as an ancillary salt (PB91) can significantly improve the efficiencies and significantly prolong the cycle life. In addition, the obtained ionic conductivities of PG, BG, and PB91 are 2.187, 1.277, and 2.124 mS cm<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, which further indicate that NaBF<sub>4</sub> as an ancillary salt (PB91) can not only stabilize the cells but also maintain a higher ionic conductivity as PG.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/asset/images/medium/am3c19126_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/asset/images/large/am3c19126_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Cycling performance of NFM//Na cells (2.0–4.2 V) at 0.2 C with 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> in G4 (PG), 1 M NaBF<sub>4</sub> in G4 (BG) and 0.9 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> + 0.1 M NaBF<sub>4</sub> in G4 (PB91) electrolytes (a); first charge and discharge profiles of NFM//Na cells with three electrolytes (b); initial cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of NFM//Na cells with a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> in three electrolytes (c), three CV curves are all from the first CV curves in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/suppl_file/am3c19126_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>; cycling performance of NFM//HC full cells with three electrolytes at 0.2 C (d); Nyquist plots of NFM//Na and HC//Na cells in three electrolytes; the NFM and HC electrodes were obtained from the disassembled NFM//HC full cells after 100 cycles at 0.2 C and then the NFM and HC electrodes were reassembled with Na electrodes to distinguish the impedance of NFM and HC electrodes (e); fitting results of <i>R</i><sub>CEI</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values of NFM//Na cells with PG, BG, and PB91 electrolytes (f).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/asset/images/large/am3c19126_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c19126&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further explore the performance of these three electrolytes in full cells, NFM//HC full cells with PG, BG, and PB91 were assembled. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d, BG and PB91 still display enhanced cycle stability and higher Coulombic efficiencies as compared to PG in NFM//HC full cells, and this phenomenon is consistent with that in NFM//Na half-cells. In order to uncover the mechanism of NaBF<sub>4</sub> on NFM//HC full cells, electrochemical impedance tests were conducted (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>e). It could be seen that the impedance of NFM cathodes cycled 100 times is much larger than that of HC anodes in the three electrolytes, indicating that the kinetics of NFM electrodes is much slower than that of HC electrodes in the full cells. BG and PB91 show higher resistance on HC electrodes than PG, which is attributed to the larger SEI film impedance formed by BF<sub>4</sub><sup>–</sup> in BG and PB91. In <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/suppl_file/am3c19126_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>, the three electrolytes show the same performance in HC//Na cells (0.01–2.0 V), and almost no capacity fading occurs after 100 cycles, which is attributed to the extremely stable structure of HC electrodes. In addition, because the failure of ether electrolytes caused by “redox shuttle” does not occur in the range of 0.01–2.0 V, the three electrolytes show high stability in HC//Na cells. To further analyze the impedance difference of NFM electrodes with the three electrolytes, the fitting results are displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>f, and the equivalent circuit used for EIS fitting is displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/suppl_file/am3c19126_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>. It was found that the CEI impedance (<i>R</i><sub>CEI</sub>) of BG (381 Ω) was significantly larger than that of PG (257 Ω), while the charge-transfer impedance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) is significantly smaller than that of PG, indicating that BG will induce a CEI layer with a larger impedance on NFM electrodes, and the CEI layer can effectively inhibit the increase of <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>. It is worth noting that <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> of PB91 is similar to that of PG, but <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> is significantly smaller than that of PG, which suggests that a small amount of NaBF<sub>4</sub> can not only obviously suppress the increase of <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> but also maintain a lower <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> value.</div><div class="NLM_p">Furthermore, self-discharge measurement (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/suppl_file/am3c19126_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S6</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a–c) and chronoamperometry tests (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d) were conducted to explore the stability of CEI and SEI layers derived from PG, BG, and PB91. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/suppl_file/am3c19126_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>, NFM//HC cells were cycled at 0.2 C with different pause times in fully charged state, and PG shows a much larger voltage drop than BG and PB91 during every pause process, which leads to a much significant capacity loss of PG than that of BG and PB91 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b). A more visual comparison of capacity loss ratio is displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c; after a 100 h pause, the capacity loss ratio of PG is as high as 64.5%, which is much higher than that of BG (13.4%) and PB91 (13.5%). These results indicate that BG and PB91 could induce more stable CEI and SEI layers, inhibiting the serious capacity loss of self-discharge. Chronoamperometry test is another effective approach to reflect the stability of CEI and SEI layers. In our previous study, it had been proved that the current at the stable state of chronoamperometry tests mainly comes from the redox shuttle effect of ether electrolytes, which to some extent reflects the rate of the side reaction. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d, when NFM//HC full cells were held at 4.0 V, the current density of BG and PB91 is much smaller than that of PG, implying that the CEI and SEI layers derived from BG and PB91 are more stable, and they can reduce the side reactions occurring in NFM//HC full cells. Transition-metals (Mn, Fe, and Ni) deposited on the cycled HC anodes (obtained from NFM//HC full cells) were tested by inductively coupled plasma (ICP) atomic emission spectrometry (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>e) and energy-dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) technique (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/suppl_file/am3c19126_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S7 and S8</a>). It could be found that the atom percent of transition metals (Mn, Fe, and Ni) in BG and PB91 is remarkably smaller than that of PG, which further declares that BG and PB91 could promote the formation of more stable CEI layers on NFM cathodes and inhibit the dissolution of transition metals.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/asset/images/medium/am3c19126_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/asset/images/large/am3c19126_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Complete cycling protocol used to measure self-discharge (a); typical performance of NFM//HC full cells cycled at 0.2 C with different pause times in fully charged state, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/suppl_file/am3c19126_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>; specific charge (red dots) and discharge (blue dots) capacity with PG, BG, and PB91 electrolytes (b); capacity loss ratio of NFM//HC full cells (c), capacity loss ratio = (1 – <i>C<sub>n</sub></i>/<i>C</i><sub><i>n</i>–1</sub>) × 100% (<i>n</i> is the number of pause steps, and <i>C</i> represents discharge specific capacity); chronoamperometry tests (d) to measure the current changes by holding the voltage of the NFM//HC full cells at 4.0 V with PG, BG, and PB91 electrolytes; content of transition metal elements (Ni, Fe, and Mn) deposited on the cycled HC anodes by ICP atomic emission spectrometry (e), HC electrodes were obtained from NFM//HC full cells cycled 50 times at 0.2 C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/asset/images/large/am3c19126_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c19126&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The properties of SEI and CEI layers have a crucial influence on the battery performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32 ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 32 and 33">(32,33)</a> Thus, SEM, XPS, EDS, and solubility test were further carried out to study the effect of NaBF<sub>4</sub> on the morphology, structure, composition, and solubility of CEI layers on the NFM (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>) and SEI layers on HC electrodes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>). As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 3</a>a and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/suppl_file/am3c19126_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S9</a>, the NFM electrode cycled 20 times in PG is visually covered by a thick layer of byproducts, which makes the boundary of NFM particles blurred. However, the surface of NFM electrodes cycled in BG and PB91 shows no obvious difference from pristine NFM electrodes. These results suggest that BG and PB91 could induce more stable CEI layers on NFM cathodes to suppress the serious decomposition of electrolytes, and the CEI layers derived from BG and PB91 are much thinner than PG.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/asset/images/medium/am3c19126_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/asset/images/large/am3c19126_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Characterization of the CEI layers on NFM cathodes. SEM image of the pristine NFM electrode and NFM electrodes cycled 20 times in NFM//HC cells at 0.2 C in PG, BG, and PB91 electrolytes (a); XPS C 1s (b), F 1s (c), and B 1s/P 2s (d) measurements of pristine NFM electrodes and NFM electrodes cycled 20 times in NFM//HC cells at 0.2 C in PG, BG, and PB91 electrolytes; peak area ratio of some organic species (C–O peak) and some inorganic species (O–C═O (CO<sub>3</sub>) and Na–F peaks) (e); atomic ratio of Ni element obtained on the surface of NFM electrodes (cycled 20 times in NFM//HC cells at 0.2 C) tested by the EDS technique (f).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/asset/images/large/am3c19126_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c19126&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/asset/images/medium/am3c19126_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/asset/images/large/am3c19126_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Characterization of the SEI layers on HC anodes. SEM image of the pristine HC electrode and HC electrodes cycled 20 times in NFM//HC cells at 0.2 C with three electrolytes (a); XPS C 1s (b), F 1s (c), and B 1s/P 2s (d) measurements of pristine HC electrodes and HC electrodes cycled 20 times in NFM//HC cells at 0.2 C in PG, BG, and PB91 electrolytes; peak area ratio of some organic species (C–O peak) and some inorganic species (O–C═O (CO<sub>3</sub>) and Na–F peaks) (e); solubility tests of SEI layers on anodes (f); capacity of Cu//Na cells (0.01–2.0 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) with a current density of 3 μA cm<sup>–2</sup>, 50, 30, 15, and 5 h pause was held after 10th, 15th, 20th, and 25th cycles, respectively, to record the capacity loss; the calculated capacity loss of PG, BG, and PB91 (g); capacity loss = <i>C<sub>n</sub></i> – <i>C</i><sub><i>n</i>–1</sub> (<i>n</i> is the number of pause steps, and <i>C</i> represents discharge capacity).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/asset/images/large/am3c19126_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c19126&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To explore the effect of NaBF<sub>4</sub> on the composition of CEI layers covered on the NFM cathode, XPS measurements were conducted (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 3</a>b–d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/suppl_file/am3c19126_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S10</a>). The C–C/C–H (284.8 eV), C–O (286.7 eV) peaks in C 1s spectrum and O–C (532.3 eV) in O 1s spectrum are mainly derived from some organic species such as RCH<sub>2</sub>ONa, Na<sub>2</sub>[(OCH<sub>2</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>O)<sub><i>n</i></sub>], and so forth. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> O–C═O (CO<sub>3</sub>) (289.3 eV) in the C 1s spectrum and O═C/Na–O (531.1 eV) in the O 1s spectrum are mainly derived from some inorganic species such as Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, and so forth. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35 ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref36" aria-label="reference 35 and 36">(35,36)</a> The Na–F (684.1 eV) peak in the F 1s spectrum can be ascribed to NaF. The B 1s spectrum is almost overlapped with the P 2s spectrum; <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> the Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>BO<sub><i>y</i></sub>F<sub><i>z</i></sub> or Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>PO<sub><i>y</i></sub>F<sub><i>z</i></sub> peak (687.2 eV) in the F 1s spectrum and B–F or P–F (198.8 eV), Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>BO<sub><i>y</i></sub>F<sub><i>z</i></sub>, or Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>PO<sub><i>y</i></sub>F<sub><i>z</i></sub> (194.9 eV) peaks in the B 1s or P 2s spectrum mainly stem from some incomplete decomposition products of NaPF<sub>6</sub> and NaBF<sub>4</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37 ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 37 and 38">(37,38)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">It could be found that C–F peaks coming from PVDF in the C 1s and F 1s spectra disappear after cycling 20 times in PG, BG, and PB91, indicating the formation of CEI layers on NFM electrodes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b,c). The C–C peaks in the pristine NFM sample stem from carbon black (Super P), but the NFM electrode cycled in PG shows a much weaker C–C peak than that in BG and PB91, suggesting that the CEI layers induced by PG are much thicker than that by BG and PB91 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b). The same conclusion could be drawn by the content of Ni element obtained from the surface of cycled NFM electrodes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 3</a>f and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/suppl_file/am3c19126_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S11</a>); a thicker CEI layer derived from PG would mask the signal of Ni element from the NFM material, thus leading to a lower Ni atomic ratio. From the B 1s/P 2s spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d), it could be observed that a strong Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>PO<sub><i>y</i></sub>F<sub><i>z</i></sub> peak is induced by PG, which means serious decomposition of NaPF<sub>6</sub>. In the BG electrolyte, an obvious Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>BO<sub><i>y</i></sub>F<sub><i>z</i></sub> peak induced by the decomposition of NaBF<sub>4</sub> could also be observed. However, the Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>PO<sub><i>y</i></sub>F<sub><i>z</i></sub>/Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>BO<sub><i>y</i></sub>F<sub><i>z</i></sub> peak in PB91 almost disappeared, which is because the content of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>BO<sub><i>y</i></sub>F<sub><i>z</i></sub> decomposed from 0.1 M NaBF<sub>4</sub> in PB91 is greatly reduced than BG, and the small amount of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>BO<sub><i>y</i></sub>F<sub><i>z</i></sub> could inhibit the decomposition of NaPF<sub>6</sub> to Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>PO<sub><i>y</i></sub>F<sub><i>z</i></sub>. This result indicates that a small amount of NaBF<sub>4</sub> would inhibit the large amount of decomposition of NaPF<sub>6</sub>. It is worth noting that the C–O peaks largely reflect the content of organic species in the CEI layers, while the O–C═O (CO<sub>3</sub>) and Na–F peaks largely represent the content of inorganic species in the CEI layers. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> To clearly show the content difference between organic (C–O peak) and inorganic species (O–C═O (CO<sub>3</sub>) and Na–F peaks) in the CEI layers, the peak area ratio of organic and inorganic species is displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e. BG and PB91 exhibit lower organic contents but higher inorganic contents when compared to PG, indicating that NaBF<sub>4</sub> could induce a CEI layer with more inorganic species. In addition, the solubility test of CEI on cathodes is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/suppl_file/am3c19126_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a>, and the capacity loss caused by the solubility of CEI layers in PG is much larger than that in BG and PB91, which suggests that CEI layers derived from BG and PB91 are more difficult to dissolve than that from PG. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> Based on the analysis of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>, ether electrolytes with NaBF<sub>4</sub> as the main salt or an ancillary salt could form a more stable CEI layer that is thin, rich in inorganic species, and difficult to dissolve.</div><div class="NLM_p">Furthermore, the characterization of SEI layers on HC anodes is also carried out (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>). After being cycled 20 times in NFM//HC cells, the surface of HC anodes in PG is also covered by large amounts of byproducts, but this phenomenon does not appear in BG and PB91, which explains that the SEI layer in PG is much thicker than that in BG and PB91 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a). The HRTEM tests displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/suppl_file/am3c19126_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a> also show that the SEI layer induced by PG is thicker than that by BG and PB91. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b, the C–O peak that mainly originated from the decomposition of the solvent in PG is much higher than that in BG and PB91, indicating that the addition of NaBF<sub>4</sub> could suppress the decomposition of the solvent. The high Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>BO<sub><i>y</i></sub>F<sub><i>z</i></sub> peaks of BG and PB91 in F 1s and B 1s spectra declare that NaBF<sub>4</sub> would be reduced to some Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>BO<sub><i>y</i></sub>F<sub><i>z</i></sub> species, which may be the key to stabilize the SEI layers (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c,d). In addition, the ratio of inorganic (O–C═O (CO<sub>3</sub>) and Na–F peaks) and organic species (C–O peak) was roughly calculated and displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>e. BG and PB91 show a lower ratio of organics and a higher ratio of inorganics, also manifesting that NaBF<sub>4</sub> could suppress the decomposition of the solvent into some organic species. Moreover, the solubility tests of SEI layers on the anodes declare that the capacity loss attributed to the SEI layer dissolution in PG is much higher than that in BG and PB91 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>f,g). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> Combining all the above conclusions in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>, the SEI layer with much Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>BO<sub><i>y</i></sub>F<sub><i>z</i></sub> species induced by NaBF<sub>4</sub> is rich in inorganics, which could inhibit the continuous decomposition of the solvent and the solubility of SEI layers.</div><div class="NLM_p">Moreover, the solvation structure of Na<sup>+</sup> in electrolytes would significantly affect the composition and structure of SEI and CEI layers. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32 ref40 ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 32, 40 and 41">(32,40,41)</a> Here, classical molecular dynamics (MD) simulations were further conducted to investigate the effect of BF<sub>4</sub><sup>–</sup> on the solvation structure in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>. The coordination number of Na–O (G4) and Na–F (PF<sub>6</sub><sup>–</sup>) is 4.89 and 1.74 in the first solvation sheath of PG (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d), but the coordination number of Na–O (G4) decreases to 3.66 and that of Na–F (BF<sub>4</sub><sup>–</sup>) increases to 2.94 in BG (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>e), indicating that BF<sub>4</sub><sup>–</sup> could decrease the coordination of G4 and Na<sup>+</sup>. The optimized Na<sup>+</sup> solvation structure in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>g also displays the same result that fewer O (G4) in BG is coordinated with Na<sup>+</sup> than PG. The obtained Na<sup>+</sup> solvation free energies in PG, BG, and PB91 are −145.47, −149.6, and −149.25 kcal mol<sup>–1</sup>, which indicate that the interaction of Na<sup>+</sup>-BF<sub>4</sub><sup>–</sup> is stronger than that of Na<sup>+</sup>-PF<sub>6</sub><sup>–</sup>, and Na<sup>+</sup> in BG and PB91 is more difficult to be desolvated than in PG (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>h). These results indicate that the stronger interaction between BF<sub>4</sub><sup>–</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> could decrease the coordination of G4 and Na<sup>+</sup>, which could increase the decomposition of anion but decrease the decomposition of G4 on the electrode/electrolyte interfaces. This is why BG and PB91 could induce an SEI/CEI rich in inorganic species, as shown in the result of XPS tests (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 3</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>).</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/asset/images/medium/am3c19126_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/asset/images/large/am3c19126_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Simulation results of PG, BG, and PB91 electrolyte models. Snapshots of the MD simulation boxes of PG (a), BG (b), and PB91 (c) electrolytes;  Na<sup>+</sup> radial distribution function (RDF) of PG (d), BG (e), and PB91 (f) electrolytes; <i>n</i>(<i>r</i>) in the right <i>Y</i>-axis means the coordination number of Na–O and Na–F; optimized Na<sup>+</sup> solvation structure of PG, BG, and PB91 electrolyte models (g); solvation free energy of the PG, BG, and PB91 electrolyte models obtained from quantum chemistry simulations (h).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/asset/images/large/am3c19126_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c19126&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectrum of PG, BG, and PB91 electrolytes was analyzed to further verify the effect of NaBF<sub>4</sub> on Na<sup>+</sup> solvation (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a). It can be seen from <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a that all three electrolytes have only one peak, indicating that there is only one chemical environment for sodium ions in the homogeneous solution in the three electrolytes. The chemical shifts of PG, BG, and PB91 samples are −2.83, −4.09, and −3.76 ppm, respectively, implying that the electron cloud density around Na<sup>+</sup> is PG &lt; PB91 &lt; BG. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> This result suggests that the addition of BF<sub>4</sub><sup>–</sup> will increase the electron cloud density around Na<sup>+</sup>, which is related to the stronger interaction between BF<sub>4</sub><sup>–</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup>. The larger polarization of BG and PB91 between the reduction and oxidation peaks also indicates the slower kinetics of BG and PB91 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c). Then, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> with a potential platform at ∼1.7 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) was selected to eliminate the interference of SEI layers, and rate capability test was carried out. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>b, the rate capacity of BG is lower than that of PG when the rate is larger than 1 C, which is attributed to the slower desolvation process. The slower desolvation process is caused by the stronger interaction between BF<sub>4</sub><sup>–</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup>, which is consistent with the result of solvation free energy (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>h). More importantly, the stronger interaction between BF<sub>4</sub><sup>–</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> could reduce the solvation of Na<sup>+</sup> with the solvent, which is the key factor for the formation of CEI and SEI layers rich in inorganic species.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/asset/images/medium/am3c19126_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/asset/images/large/am3c19126_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Characterization of the solvation and gas evolution tests. <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectrum of PG, BG, and PB91 electrolytes (a); rate capacity of Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (with 1.7 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na platform)//Na cells (1.5–2.0 V) with PG, BG, and PB91 electrolytes (1 C = 60 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) (b); gas evolution of NFM//HC cells with PG (c), BG (d), and PB91 (e); the full cells were charged to 4.0 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) at 0.2C, and the voltage was kept constant for some hours. Note that the left/right <i>y</i>-axis label only appears in the far-left/right figure, and the gassing rate units of all gases are nmol min<sup>–1</sup> mg<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/asset/images/large/am3c19126_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c19126&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The gas evolution of NFM//HC cells with three electrolytes is displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>c–e. H<sub>2</sub> is found to release when NFM//HC starts charging in three electrolytes, which is attributed to the electrochemical reduction of trace water (HF or ROH) in electrolytes (H<sub>2</sub>O/ROH/HF + e<sup>–</sup> → OH<sup>–</sup>/RO<sup>–</sup>/F<sup>–</sup> + 1/2 H<sub>2</sub>↑). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a> A smaller H<sub>2</sub> evolution rate in BG and PB91 than that in PG could be found, which is because more stable SEI films on HC derived from BG and PB91 could inhibit the reduction of H<sub>2</sub>O (HF or ROH). It is worth noting that PG begins producing a large amount of CH<sub>4</sub> and C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub> at 3.95 V, which is proved to be derived from the redox shuttle effect of PG in our previous published work. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> However, the gassing rate of CH<sub>4</sub> and C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub> in BG and PB91 is reduced greatly, further implying that the stable CEI and SEI layers induced by NaBF<sub>4</sub> on NFM cathodes and HC anodes could suppress the gas evolution of NFM//HC cells.</div><div class="NLM_p">Based on all of the above analyses, the scheme of solvation structures and electrode/electrolyte interfaces in PG and BG electrolytes is constructed and displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>. BF<sub>4</sub><sup>–</sup> shows the stronger interaction with Na<sup>+</sup> than PF<sub>6</sub><sup>–</sup>, and the stronger interaction between BF<sub>4</sub><sup>–</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> could reduce the solvation of Na<sup>+</sup> with the solvent, thus inducing the formation of CEI and SEI layers rich in inorganic species. Moreover, the CEI and SEI layers with many inorganic species induced by BG are thinner and more stable, which could inhibit the continuous decomposition of solvent and the solubility of SEI layers.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/asset/images/medium/am3c19126_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/asset/images/large/am3c19126_0007.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. Scheme of solvation structures and electrode/electrolyte interfaces in PG (a) and BG (b).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c19126/asset/images/large/am3c19126_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c19126&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, NaBF<sub>4</sub> as the main salt or additive in ether electrolytes could effectively improve the cycling performance and efficiencies in the NFM//HC full cells. In addition, self-discharge measurement and chronoamperometry tests were also conducted and showed that BG and PB91 could inhibit the serious self-discharge capacity loss and the side reaction occurring on NFM cathodes with PG. <sup>23</sup>Na NMR spectrum and rate capability test indicated that the interaction between BF<sub>4</sub><sup>–</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup> is stronger than that between PF<sub>6</sub><sup>–</sup> and Na<sup>+</sup>, which could reduce the solvation of Na<sup>+</sup> with the solvent, and this is the key factor for the formation of CEI and SEI layers rich in inorganic species. The morphology, structure, composition, and solubility of CEI and SEI layers were explored by SEM, XPS, EDS, and solubility tests, and it was concluded that NaBF<sub>4</sub> could induce more stable and thinner electrode/electrolyte interfaces rich in B-containing species and inorganics. This work breaks the inertial idea of using NaPF<sub>6</sub> as an electrolyte salt and improves the feasibility of applying ether-based electrolytes in sodium ion full cells.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2023</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>6</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">e12633</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Optimization Strategies Toward Functional Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chen, Jingwei; Adit, Gupta; Li, Lun; Zhang, Yingxin; Chua, Daniel H. C.; Lee, Pooi See</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2023</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Exploration of alternative energy storage systems has been more than necessary in view of the supply risks haunting lithium-ion batteries.  Among various alternative electrochem. energy storage devices, sodium-ion battery outstands with advantages of cost-effectiveness and comparable energy d. with lithium-ion batteries.  Thanks to the similar electrochem. mechanism, the research and development of lithium-ion batteries have forged a solid foundation for sodium-ion battery explorations.  Advancements in sodium-ion batteries have been witnessed in terms of superior electrochem. performance and broader application scenarios.  Here, the strategies adopted to optimize the battery components (cathode, anode, electrolyte, separator, binder, current collector, etc.) and the cost, safety, and commercialization issues in sodium-ion batteries are summarized and discussed.  Based on these optimization strategies, assembly of functional (flexible, stretchable, self-healable, and self-chargeable) and integrated sodium-ion batteries (-actuators, -sensors, electrochromic, etc.) have been realized.  Despite these achievements, challenges including energy d., scalability, trade-off between energy d. and functionality, cost, etc. are to be addressed for sodium-ion battery commercialization.  This review aims at providing an overview of the up-to-date achievements in sodium-ion batteries and serves to inspire more efforts in designing upgraded sodium-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGogiPMxi_Y61bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0livhca9m0_wKQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3sXpvFShsbY%253D&amp;md5=0d50fba27e49580cd9941f3f59be2404</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1039/C6CS00776G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hwang</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Myung</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span>. <i>Chem. Soc. Rev.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>46</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">12</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">3529</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">3614</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3529-3614</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CSRVBR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0306-0012</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGot-2IKXj-Q-7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0livhca9m0_wKQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXkvF2rsbk%253D&amp;md5=cfc7b2d153696dcbded22d954ff06174</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1016/j.jallcom.2023.170486"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tianwei, Yu</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">The research and industrialization progress and prospects of sodium ion battery</span>. <i>J. Alloys Compd.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2023</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>958</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">170486</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.jallcom.2023.170486</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D958%26publication_year%3D2023%26journal%3DJ.%2BAlloys%2BCompd.%26author%3DYu%2BTianwei%26title%3DThe%2Bresearch%2Band%2Bindustrialization%2Bprogress%2Band%2Bprospects%2Bof%2Bsodium%2Bion%2Bbattery%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.jallcom.2023.170486&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.3c19126&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.jallcom.2023.170486&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord empty" id="
                            cas_R
                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1021/acsmaterialslett.0c00356"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Niu</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yin</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Manipulating Electrode/Electrolyte Interphases of Sodium-Ion Batteries: Strategies and Perspectives. ACS</span>. <i>Mater. Lett.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2021</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>3</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">1</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">18</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">41</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Manipulating Electrode/Electrolyte Interphases of Sodium-Ion Batteries: Strategies and Perspectives</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Enhui; Niu, Yubin; Yin, Ya-Xia; Guo, Yu-Guo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Materials Letters</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  After the past decade's rapid development, the com. demands for sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have been put on the schedule for large-scale energy storage.  Even though the electrode-electrolyte interphases play a very important role in detg. the overall battery performance in terms of high energy d. and long-cycling stability, studies regarding their fundamental understanding and regulation strategies are still in their infancy.  Herein, we comprehensively review the current research status and the challenging issues of the as-generated SIB interphases from three main aspects.  Firstly, a fundamental understanding of the main body interphase layers is introduced through the development of their formation mechanism, their compn./structure, and the dynamic evolution process involved, all of which are highly responsible for the Na+ ion transport behavior to det. the final kinetic diffusion.  Then, interphase manipulation via the parental electrolyte is summarized in terms of electrolyte engineering strategies, such as the solvent/salt selection, the concn. effect, and the functional additive screening to build a more stable interphase layer for desirable electrochem. reversibility.  Finally, potential effects from the chosen electrodes are discussed to provide necessary assocns. with the interphase formation and evolution.  Crit. challenges for building stable Na-based interphase are identified, and in particular, new ways of thinking about the interphase chem. and the electrolyte chem. based on SIBs, are strongly appealing.  We believe that this work is likely to attract attention to the rational design of Na-based interphase layers towards high-energy and long-life-span batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqVuJ3ZWCnEX7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj5rGvXSQHMlg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXisVWntb3J&amp;md5=5cc0e78592ac0fc5cb0e24007ba54fa6</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201808393"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, Zhao</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Xie</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrolytes and Electrolyte/Electrode Interfaces in Sodium-Ion Batteries: From Scientific Research to Practical Application</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrolytes and Interphases in Sodium-Based Rechargeable Batteries: Recent Advances and Perspectives</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Eshetu, Gebrekidan Gebresilassie; Elia, Giuseppe Antonio; Armand, Michel; Forsyth, Maria; Komaba, Shinichi; Rojo, Teofilo; Passerini, Stefano</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
